Question 1

  1. Write code that extracts the article html from https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/ and dumps it to a .pkl (or other appropriate file)
In[]:
import requests
import pickle
from bs4 import BeautifulSoup

#url of website
url="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/"

#Fetch the page
response = requests.get(url)

#Store file name
filename = 'article-html.pkl'

#Check if the response was successful
if response.status_code==200:
    html_content = response.content
    #Parse the content
    data = BeautifulSoup(html_content, 'html.parser')
    #Write to a file
    with open(filename,'wb') as file:
        pickle.dump(html_content, file)
        print(f"The contents have been written to {filename}")
else:
    print('Failed to retrieve the web page.')

The contents have been written to article-html.pkl
In[19]:
#Check for valid HTML content  
def is_valid_html(content):

    if not content:
        return False
    soup = BeautifulSoup(content, 'html.parser')
    return bool(soup.find())

def read_and_validate_pickle(file_path): 
    try: 
        with open(file_path, 'rb') as file: 
            data = pickle.load(file) 
            if is_valid_html(data): 
                print("Valid HTML content") 
                print(data) 
            else: 
                print("Invalid HTML content. Using default template.") 
                data = "<html><body>This is a default template.</body></html>" 
                print(data) 
    except Exception as e: 
        print(f"Error reading pickle file: {e}") 

# Example usage
read_and_validate_pickle('article-html.pkl')

     <main id="main" class="site-main" role="main">\n\n        \n            \n<article itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Article" id="post-466450" class="post-466450 post type-post status-publish format-standard has-post-thumbnail hentry category-car-hacks category-engineering category-featured category-laser-hacks category-slider tag-laser tag-laser-headlight tag-laser-headlights tag-light">\n    <header class="entry-header">\n        <h1 class="entry-title" itemprop="name">How Laser Headlights Work</h1>\n        <div class="entry-meta">\n            <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comments" class="comments-counts"><span class="icon-hackaday icon-hackaday-comment"></span>\n                130 Comments            </a>\n            <ul class="meta-authors vcard author">\n                <li>by:</li>\n                <span class="fn"><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/author/lewinday/" title="Posts by Lewin Day" class="author url fn" rel="author">Lewin Day</a></span>\n            </ul>\n        </div><!-- .entry-meta -->\n\n        <div class="entry-meta entry-meta-last">\n            <span class="entry-date published updated" itemprop="datePublished" content="March 22, 2021"><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/" title="March 22, 2021 - 7:01 am">March 22, 2021</a></span>\n            <ul class="share-post">\n                <li><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://www.facebook.com/sharer/sharer.php?u=https%3A%2F%2Fhackaday.com%2F2021%2F03%2F22%2Fhow-laser-headlights-work%2F" target="_blank"><span class="icon-hackaday icon-hackaday-facebook"></span></a></li>\n                <li><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://twitter.com/intent/tweet?text=How+Laser+Headlights+Work via @hackaday&amp;url=https%3A%2F%2Fhackaday.com%2F2021%2F03%2F22%2Fhow-laser-headlights-work%2F" target="_blank"><span class="icon-hackaday icon-hackaday-twitter"></span></a></li>\n            </ul>\n        </div><!-- .entry-meta -->\n\n        <div class="entry-featured-image"><img itemprop="image" content="https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/LaserHeadlight-thumb.jpg?w=600&amp;h=600" src="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/LaserHeadlight.jpg?w=800" alt=""></div>\n    </header><!-- .entry-header -->\n\n    <div class="entry-content" itemprop="articleBody">\n        <p>When we think about the onward march of automotive technology, headlights aren&#8217;t usually the first thing that come to mind. Engines, fuel efficiency, and the switch to electric power are all more front of mind. However, that doesn&#8217;t mean there aren&#8217;t thousands of engineers around the world working to improve the state of the art in automotive lighting day in, day out.</p>\n<p>Sealed beam headlights gave way to more modern designs once regulations loosened up, while bulbs moved from simple halogens to xenon HIDs and, more recently, LEDs. Now, a new technology is on the scene, with lasers!</p>\n<p><span id="more-466450"></span></p>\n<h2>Laser Headlights?!</h2>\n<figure id="attachment_466463" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-466463" style="width: 290px" class="wp-caption alignright"><img data-attachment-id="466463" data-permalink="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/laser-light-bwm-proto-2/" data-orig-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-light-bwm-proto-2.jpg" data-orig-size="290,193" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="laser light bwm proto 2" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-light-bwm-proto-2.jpg?w=290" data-large-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-light-bwm-proto-2.jpg?w=290" loading="lazy" class="size-medium wp-image-466463" src="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-light-bwm-proto-2.jpg?w=290" alt="" width="290" height="193" srcset="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-light-bwm-proto-2.jpg 290w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-light-bwm-proto-2.jpg?resize=250,166 250w" sizes="(max-width: 290px) 100vw, 290px"/><figcaption id="caption-attachment-466463" class="wp-caption-text">BWM&#8217;s prototype laser headlight assemblies undergoing testing.</figcaption></figure>\n<p>The first image brought to mind by the phrase &#8220;laser headlights&#8221; is that of laser beams firing out the front of an automobile. Obviously, coherent beams of monochromatic light would make for poor illumination outside of a very specific spot quite some distance away. Thankfully for our eyes, laser headlights don&#8217;t work in this way at all.</p>\n<p>Instead, laser headlights consist of one or more solid state laser diodes mounted inside the headlight. These blue lasers are fired at a yellow phosphor, similar to that used in white LEDs. This produces a powerful, vibrant white light that can then be bounced off reflectors and out of the headlights towards the road. Laser headlights built in this way have several benefits. They&#8217;re more energy efficient than LEDs that put out the same amount of light, while also being more space efficient, too.</p>\n<figure id="attachment_466464" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-466464" style="width: 400px" class="wp-caption alignleft"><img data-attachment-id="466464" data-permalink="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/laser-headlight-bmw-i8/" data-orig-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg" data-orig-size="1900,1265" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="laser headlight bmw i8" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?w=400" data-large-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?w=800" loading="lazy" class="size-medium wp-image-466464" src="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?w=400" alt="" width="400" height="266" srcset="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg 1900w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?resize=250,166 250w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?resize=400,266 400w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?resize=800,533 800w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?resize=1536,1023 1536w" sizes="(max-width: 400px) 100vw, 400px"/><figcaption id="caption-attachment-466464" class="wp-caption-text">BWM&#8217;s futuristic i8 was one of the first vehicles to ship with laser headlight technology.</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Laser headlights are still a nascent technology, thus far only appearing in a few BMW, Audi, and other select vehicles. <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://www.osram.com/am/specials/trends-in-automotive-lighting/laser-light-new-headlight-technology/questions-and-answers-on-innovative-laser-technology/index.jsp" target="_blank">BMW&#8217;s technology was developed in partnership with lighting experts OSRAM</a>. In practice, a regular LED low-beam lamp is used, with the laser used to create an incredibly bright and focused spot, used for high-beams. This can provide illumination out to 600 meters ahead of the vehicle, double that of conventional LED high beams. The lights use indium gallium nitride diode lasers that were originally used in projectors, with power levels above 1 watt. One of the challenges in implementing such technology in an automotive environment is the need for it to operate at temperature extremes. While research lasers and laser pointers may primarily be used at typical room temperatures, automotive headlights must be able to withstand everything from 40 degrees below zero up to 50 degrees C. Thankfully, the laser&#8217;s high efficiency means it doesn&#8217;t have huge heat output of its own to further complicate things. Other engineering challenges involve tailoring the optics package for the rough-and-tumble, high vibration environment found in the automotive application. It&#8217;s also important to ensure, as with any such device, that the end user can&#8217;t be exposed to harmful laser radiation in the event of accident or malfunction.</p>\n<h2>Tearing Down the Laser Headlight</h2>\n<figure id="attachment_466457" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-466457" style="width: 400px" class="wp-caption alignright"><img data-attachment-id="466457" data-permalink="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/ledheadlights-aliexp/" data-orig-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg" data-orig-size="930,930" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="ledheadlights-aliexp" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg?w=400" data-large-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg?w=625" loading="lazy" class="size-medium wp-image-466457" src="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg?w=400" alt="" width="400" height="400" srcset="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg 930w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg?resize=250,250 250w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg?resize=400,400 400w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg?resize=625,625 625w" sizes="(max-width: 400px) 100vw, 400px"/><figcaption id="caption-attachment-466457" class="wp-caption-text">A marketing image showing the construction of an aftermarket LED/laser headlight. We&#8217;d take the laser power with a grain of salt &#8212; it&#8217;s difficult to imagine a 10 W laser shining directly on some small LEDs without melting a hole through the board in short order.</figcaption></figure>\n<p>An aftermarket has sprung up too, with delightfully innovative designs. Combined laser/LED headlights are readily available on Alibaba, <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://www.aliexpress.com/item/1005001889968976.html?spm=a2g0o.search0302.0.0.5bebc1d45ruE7k&amp;algo_pvid=b016ca5c-d0fe-4309-ab5f-e39ea8770c80&amp;algo_expid=b016ca5c-d0fe-4309-ab5f-e39ea8770c80-0&amp;btsid=0b0a556516155071180373533e897a&amp;ws_ab_test=searchweb0_0,searchweb201602_,searchweb201603_" target="_blank">designed as a drop in replacement for projector lamps on existing vehicles.</a> These often use an LED low-beam, and a combined LED/laser high beam, where the laser diode shoots directly at the LED phosphor to excite it further, rather than using its own. These units often also come with fan cooling to keep the laser and LEDs below their maximum operational temperature. Such developments are exciting, though it&#8217;s important to be wary of the performance of unknown aftermarket headlights. <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://jalopnik.com/why-most-led-headlight-upgrades-dont-really-work-an-ex-1843070472" target="_blank">Many aftermarket LED headlight &#8220;upgrades&#8221; fail to pass muster</a> when it comes to real-world performance, and there&#8217;s no reason to believe hybrid LED/laser designs will be any different. We&#8217;d love to pass a selection of these parts through <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/http://www.vdrsyd.com/ancap_datasheets/xprotocol_archive/IIHS/headlight_test_rating_protocol.pdf" target="_blank">a full IIHS test protocol</a>, but that&#8217;s sadly beyond the scope (and budget!) of this article.</p>\n<p>However, [mikeselectricstuff] has happened to lay his hands on both the BMW and aftermarket parts, <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7fRjMHtnShs" target="_blank">tearing them all down in his workshop to see what makes them tick.</a> The differences are multitude when laid bare on the bench. The AliExpress part is relatively simple, wired up no differently from a regular headlight. Interestingly, however, the laser high-beam circuit runs all the time in these parts. To prevent blinding other road users, a shutter is kept in place to block the light, which is moved out of the way with a solenoid when the driver turns on the high beam switch.</p>\n<p>Where the aftermarket part is a little out of left field, the BMW design is another thing entirely. The cutting-edge headlights are hooked up with multiple connectors and over 30 conductors, with much of the driver electronics living in an external controller. Much of this is to drive the various LEDs and stepper motors for slewing the headlights when steering. However, the laser assembly brings its own complexities. Twin light sensors are built inside to monitor the laser beam, and a special metal blocking arm sits directly in front of the diode, presumably to stop the laser light leaving the headlight in the event the phosphor coating burns through. It&#8217;s truly wild to get a look inside a modern luxury car&#8217;s headlight and see just how far we&#8217;ve come from the old days of simple sealed beams.</p>\n<h2>Cost Versus Performance</h2>\n<p>Despite the efficiency gains available, the technology remains expensive. Powerful laser diodes don&#8217;t come cheap, after all. However, as the technology trickles down to lower-end models, it&#8217;s likely that we could see economies of scale change that for the better. Indeed, if national authorities begin to demand higher performance headlights as standard, we could see laser headlights become the norm, rather than an expensive luxury. The technology could naturally be applied to home and commercial lighting, too &#8212; though we suspect the potential gains are limited enough that LED lighting will remain the norm for some time to come.</p>\n<figure id="attachment_466459" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-466459" style="width: 400px" class="wp-caption alignright"><img data-attachment-id="466459" data-permalink="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/laser-headlights-bmw-laser/" data-orig-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlights-bmw-laser.jpg" data-orig-size="796,316" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="laser headlights bmw laser" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlights-bmw-laser.jpg?w=400" data-large-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlights-bmw-laser.jpg?w=796" loading="lazy" class="size-medium wp-image-466459" id="caption-attachment-466459" class="wp-caption-text">The high light output of laser headlights in a compact package allows engineers greater freedom when designing the front-end of a car.</figcaption></figure>\n<p>As it currently stands, much of the appeal of the new technology is about the packaging benefits, which allow automotive designers greater freedom around the headlight area. Such concerns are less of a factor when it comes to light fittings in the home or office, or indeed, on lower-end automobiles. Regardless, it&#8217;s an exciting new application for lasers and one we&#8217;ll be sure to see more of in the future.</p>\n<p>&nbsp;</p>\n\t            </div><!-- .entry-content -->\n    <ul But the main issue I find is how harsh they are on other drivers vision.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333286"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333286\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333286\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t<div class="reply"><a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/?replytocom=6333286#respond" data-commentid="6333286" data-postid="466450" data-belowelement="div-comment-6333286" data-respondelement="respond" data-replyto="Reply to Ostracus" aria-label="Reply to Ostracus">Reply</a></div>\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t<ol class="children">\n\t<li id="comment-6333309" class="comment odd alt depth-2 parent ">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333309" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Ken Boak</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333309">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-22T08:16:04-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 22, 2021 at 8:16 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>As someone in my 50s, with naturally deteriorating eyesight, I am frequently dazzled by insanely bright HID or LED headlights.</p>\n<p>Laser excited phosphor headlights just sound like an tech overcomplication for up market German performance cars. Most people don&#8217;t want to, or are unlikely to ever drive at 240km/h at night on a German autobahn.</p>\n<p>Light emissions from these vehicles can be just as offensive as their tail-pipe emissions.</p>\n<p>If the designers are bored and want to put more tech into vehicles just for the sake of it, they might consider an infrared floodlight and an IR camera connected to a head-up-display. </p>\n<p>If you need to see 600m ahead of you in the dark &#8211; you are probably driving too fast.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333309"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333309\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333309\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t<div class="reply"><a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/?replytocom=6333309#respond" data-commentid="6333309" data-postid="466450" data-belowelement="div-comment-6333309" data-respondelement="respond" data-replyto="Reply to Ken Boak" aria-label="Reply to Ken Boak">Reply</a></div>\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t<ol class="children">\n\t<li id="comment-6333318" class="comment even depth-3 parent ">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333318" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Starfix</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333318">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-22T08:30:32-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 22, 2021 at 8:30 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>To be complete, these laser headlamps comes with a, intelligent driver that enable laser only under conditions (speed, steering wheel, no other car crossing).<br/>\nBut in my own opinion, with these restrictions you can see at 600m when&#8230; there&#8217;s nothing to see. And who cares what&#8217;s happening at 600m ?</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333318"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333318\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333318\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t<div class="reply"><a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/?replytocom=6333318#respond" data-commentid="6333318" data-postid="466450" data-belowelement="div-comment-6333318" data-respondelement="respond" data-replyto="Reply to Starfix" aria-label="Reply to Starfix">Reply</a></div>\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t<ol class="children">\n\t<li id="comment-6333336" class="comment odd alt depth-4 parent ">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333336" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Pat</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333336">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-22T09:06:59-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 22, 2021 at 9:06 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>I&#8217;m&#8230; just trying to parse this. You really think there&#8217;s no point to having vision out to 600 m (30 seconds at 70 kph) at night when there are no other cars on the road? There are things on roads other than cars, you know.</p>\n<p>A hard brake at 70 kph will take ~15 second to come to a stop, and carries a *big* risk if you&#8217;re the lead car with someone behind you. This basically *doubles* the visibility distance.</p>\n<p>I mean, yes, they&#8217;re a problem for oncoming traffic and drivers in front of you. That&#8217;s why they&#8217;re high beams.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333336"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333336\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333336\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t<div class="reply"><a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/?replytocom=6333336#respond" data-commentid="6333336" data-postid="466450" data-belowelement="div-comment-6333336" data-respondelement="respond" data-replyto="Reply to Pat" aria-label="Reply to Pat">Reply</a></div>\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t<ol class="children">\n\t<li id="comment-6333351" class="comment even depth-5  max-depth">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333351" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Brakes</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333351">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-22T09:54:22-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 22, 2021 at 9:54 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>A full stop from 70 KPH takes FIFTEEN SECONDS?  You need to get your brakes checked&#8230;</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333351"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333351\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333351\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n\t<li id="comment-6333353" class="comment odd alt depth-5  max-depth">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333353" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Dude</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333353">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-22T10:04:51-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 22, 2021 at 10:04 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>I don&#8217;t think so. An average car can do a hard stop within 70 meters from 70 kph (19.5 m/s) and that&#8217;s giving you two seconds of reaction time. Assuming linear deceleration, your average stopping time will be between 7-8 seconds. If you take twice that time, your brakes are shot.</p>\n<p>Second point is, assuming average eyesight, the smallest distinct object you can just about make out from 600 meters away would be the size of a football, if it has enough contrast with the background, but that would require daylight levels of illumination, and no vibration and shaking from the vehicle. In the dark, no matter how bright your headlights are, you would be lucky to make out an object the size of a truck at that distance. It&#8217;s just gonna be a blur.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333353"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333353\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333353\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n\t<li id="comment-6333356" class="comment even depth-5  max-depth">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333356" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">asdf asdf</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333356">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-22T10:13:03-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 22, 2021 at 10:13 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>A lot of cars can do a 60-0mph stop in less than 200 feet. The heck are you driving for it to take 7-8 seconds?</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333356"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333356\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333356\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n\t<li id="comment-6333364" class="comment odd alt depth-5  max-depth">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333364" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Pat</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333364">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-22T10:40:57-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 22, 2021 at 10:40 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>&#8220;Assuming linear deceleration, your average stopping time will be between 7-8 seconds. If you take twice that time, your brakes are shot.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Unless the road&#8217;s wet, in which case the general estimate is a factor of two increase in stopping distance, which hey, gets you to around that 15 second mark (okay, maybe 12-13 seconds, I did put an &#8216;approximate&#8217; there). You don&#8217;t design safety for the *best* conditions. Should&#8217;ve clarified that better, though.</p>\n<p>&#8220;Second point is, assuming average eyesight, the smallest distinct object you can just about make out from 600 meters away would be the size of a football&#8221;</p>\n<p>&#8230; I&#8217;m sorry, do you generally worry about hitting footballs on the road? I&#8217;m pretty sure the main things I worry about on roads (deer, vehicles, trees) are *way* bigger than footballs.</p>\n<p>Besides, the main point isn&#8217;t seeing things at 600 m. It&#8217;s seeing things farther than ~200 m away, which is where normal high beams cut out.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333364"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333364\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333364\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n\t<li id="comment-6333365" class="comment even depth-5  max-depth">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333365" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Pat</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333365">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-22T10:42:15-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 22, 2021 at 10:42 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>&#8220;A lot of cars can do a 60-0mph stop in less than 200 feet. illuminate in that area, and about half my fellow LED lighting engineers are roughly as avid cyclists as I am.  We are thinking about and working on this.  In the US, the NTSB and DOT have functional requirements for automotive lighting that includes not blinding oncoming traffic.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333493"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333493\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333493\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t<div class="reply"><a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/?replytocom=6333493#respond" data-commentid="6333493" data-postid="466450" data-belowelement="div-comment-6333493" data-respondelement="respond" data-replyto="Reply to smellsofbikes" aria-label="Reply to smellsofbikes">Reply</a></div>\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t<ol class="children">\n\t<li id="comment-6333509" class="comment even depth-5  max-depth">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333509" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Mike</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333509">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-22T16:41:27-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 22, 2021 at 4:41 pm\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>None of these &#8220;save the other drivers&#8221; technologies are good enough, at least not yet.  And based on the complaints here and elsewhere, it seems clear to me that the NHTSA, NTSB and DOT have all failed at their jobs.</p>\n<p>If these &#8220;functional&#8221; requirements don&#8217;t have metrics attached, isn&#8217;t it just someone&#8217;s subjectivity making the decisions?  Note the metrics that did/do exist for halogen lights are not sufficient in protecting other drivers from these new headlight technologies. New regulations are needed to address this increasingly complicated landscape of headlights.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333509"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333509\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333509\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n\t<li id="comment-6333802" class="comment odd alt depth-5  max-depth">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333802" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">three_d_dave</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333802">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-23T14:34:58-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 23, 2021 at 2:34 pm\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>It would be great if they worked. So far the largest problem is that the lamps are bright enough that sufficient light is reflected down from the windshield to the dash and back to the windshield to leave very low contrast ahead of me from cars behind me. And they are always shown on desert-flat roads, without any consideration for cresting hills. </p>\n<p>But it&#8217;s great that you are working on this because the razor edge cut-off that drives 0% to 100% to 0% to 100% due to slight bumps in the road that wipe out all my night vision are really appreciated. Those DOT requirements are whatever the industry bought and paid for SAE specification says it is, and it says nothing about things like blinding amounts of light reflected up from wet roads.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333802"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333802\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333802\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n\t<li id="comment-6333816" class="comment even depth-5  max-depth">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333816" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Marty</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333816">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-23T15:21:25-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 23, 2021 at 3:21 pm\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>There is a demo of a system like this that used a DPL projector and camera that took this idea to the extreme. .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>Could the problem be poorly designed aftermarket LEDs?</p>\n<p>Low beams should have a solid cutoff line that&#8217;s about hood level, assuming the headlights are properly aimed.  </p>\n<p>I&#8217;ve seen a lot of aftermarket bulbs that won&#8217;t focus properly. .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>The arm is fixed, and is almost certainly hefty enough to absorb 1-10w if laser energy without heating up all that much, so no burn through. Also, there&#8217;s no misalignment if the the laser housing is placed in a premachined or cast part with no option for adjusting the alignment. So basically as is designed, if the phosphor coating is destroyed and the arm isn&#8217;t a mirror, then all that happens is the lasert light gets scattered around the headlight interior and at best some leaks out but due to light spreading out, unless you where right up on the spot where the phosphor was you wouldn&#8217;t get enough laser energy hitting your eye to do any damage.</p>\n<p>Stop worrying about imagined danger when there isn&#8217;t any.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>With the vibrations, poor maintenance and how little laser energy it needs to damage the eye I&#8217;d say there is a very real danger still. If you can be 100% certain the heatsinking and orientation of the this blocking arm you are not putting it in a car in the real world, where the sudden shock of hitting a pot hole or curb and the generally harsh environment with constant vibrations of a bad road has broken things its not supposed to be able to many many times now&#8230;</p>\n<p>I&#8217;m sure its perfectly safe as a technology in the lab, will be fine in cars for a while, but at some point the housing will crack/bend, the arm will corrode, water can ingress etc etc, and being an invisible problem there will be stray lasers that can cause serious eye damage out there, because nobody noticed the failure yet (when its throwing dazzingly bright light hundreds of meters by design even shedding a sizeable percentage won&#8217;t be obvious to the driver much of the time, and cracked parts or water inside are not always visible without complete disassembly and possibly x-rays either)</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- If I&#8217;m ever forced to own a car with them then my first &#8216;customization&#8217; will involve diagonal cutters. Failing that, scuffing the lenses with sandpaper, followed by a coating of steel-filled epoxy.</p>\n<p>Effective and safe automotive illumination was a solved problem 30 years ago, and most subsequent &#8216;improvements&#8217; represent a triumph of style over substance. Even a lot of LED lighting is a distracting and annoying nuisance, except in those instances where the PWM frequency is high enough where my eyes see only a faint flicker instead of the illusion that the lights are jumping around in mid-air.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6334611"><a I already have problems with many HID headlights that are from such a focused beam of light that they are piercing at night and nearly blind me with oncoming traffic. I have to forcefully look away to not be blinded by all those new types of searchlights lower safety significantly. And then of course the headlights that are adjusted too high because of load in the back of the car. I&#8217;m happy my car has sealed beam lights and is auto leveling.<br/>\nBut yes. Those bmw&#8217;s, audi&#8217;s, vw&#8217;s, volvos and suvs are a safety hazard for the rest of the cars around them.<br/>\nIf i&#8217;m in a bad mood, I just turn on my big lights if one is approaching me.</p>\n<p>How many lumens is enough you think.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>Why should one use UV when intense blue light is available? UV has higher quantum energy than blue, which has to be dropped by the fluorescent phosphor to different VIS wavelengths. So blue can be more efficient than UV  at the stage of the phosphor. Not to mention: blue LEDs are very efficient, UV LEDS are not.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span I have been cussing the led lights the past  10 years with the last 4 years really being a living hell driving in the dark during rush hours. My solution was to tone those car windows that the law permits over here and additonally gluing plastic toning films on the side windows so the films occultate the side mirrors when driving. An imperfect solution but in a pinch i can slide down the side windows and have normal mirror brightness when reversing in those dark winter months. \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333740"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/?replytocom=6333740#respond" data-commentid="6333740" data-postid="466450" data-belowelement="div-comment-6333740" data-respondelement="respond" data-replyto="Reply to Dude" aria-label="Reply to Dude">Reply</a></div>\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n</ol><!-- .children -->\n</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n</ol><!-- .children -->\n</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n\t<li id="comment-6333806" class="comment even depth-2  ">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333806" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">three_d_dave</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333806">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-23T15:00:02-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 23, 2021 at 3:00 pm\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>Today they are for high beams. Then it will be for &#8220;safety&#8221; that it is transitioned to low beams. </p>\n<p>Recall that the SAE standards for low-beams assumed that the maximum illumination would be in the central area aligned with the filament, with fall-off to the edges to avoid a sharp transition, and then lamp makers used optics to drive the maximum allowable illumination to 100% of the field with no gradual drop-off, creating that blinding flash transition so popular today. Of course they also did that test with the mandated 12V supply even though autos run theirs at 14-15V, for a gain of around 20-40% in brightness over the test condition. At least with LEDs that lie no longer works, but the elimination of gradual falloff more than makes up. And, the original lamps had a spectrum with little blue in it; now that component that destroys night vision, is a large component.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333806"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333806\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333806\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t<div class="reply"><a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/?replytocom=6333806#respond" data-commentid="6333806" data-postid="466450" data-belowelement="div-comment-6333806" data-respondelement="respond" data-replyto="Reply to three_d_dave" aria-label="Reply to three_d_dave">Reply</a></div>\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n</ol><!-- .children -->\n</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n\t<li id="comment-6333342" class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 parent ">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333342" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Alice</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333342">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-22T09:23:31-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 22, 2021 at 9:23 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>Since when efficiency is a problem? .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>Seriously though. Air resistance starts to dominate over rolling resistance around 20 mph and up.</p>\n<p>In stop/go traffic, you&#8217;re never going very fast. Each acceleration to 25 mph in a two-tonne car takes about 120 kJ of energy, which is worth 6 seconds of driving on the highway at 20 kW (27 HP) average power. Most of the fuel consumption is due to idle loss, which consumes about 1-2 kW to keep the belts turning while you&#8217;re waiting at the red lights.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content That 44% figure is <b>efficacy</b>, not efficiency. That means it is <i>relative to an single-color 550nm (yellow-green) light source</i> and you wouldn&#8217;t want to light a room with that anyway.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6334871"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6334871\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6334871\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t<div class="reply"><a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/?replytocom=6334871#respond" data-commentid="6334871" data-postid="466450" data-belowelement="div-comment-6334871" data-respondelement="respond" data-replyto="Reply to rnjacobs" aria-label="Reply to rnjacobs">Reply</a></div>\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t<ol class="children">\n\t<li id="comment-6334949" class="comment even depth-4 parent ">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6334949" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Dude</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6334949">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-27T05:52:43-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 27, 2021 at 5:52 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>No. You got it exactly wrong the last time. Efficacy refers to the number of lumens per Watt of the radiation, and luminous efficiency refers to the perceived brightness relative to 555 nm light. Then there is the overall luminous efficacy and efficiency of the source:</p>\n<p><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luminous_efficacy#Lighting_efficiency" rel="nofollow ugc" target="_blank">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luminous_efficacy#Lighting_efficiency</a></p>\n<p>&#8220;The main difference between the luminous efficacy of radiation and the luminous efficacy of a source is that the latter accounts for input energy that is lost as heat or otherwise&#8221;<br/>\n&#8220;The following table lists luminous efficacy of a source and efficiency for various light sources&#8221;</p>\n<p>&#8220;Theoretical limit for a white LED with phosphorescence color mixing 260\xe2\x80\x93300 lm/W 38.1\xe2\x80\x9343.9%&#8221; </p>\n<p>This IS counting &#8220;watts photons visible light out  \xc3\xb7 watts electrons in&#8221;.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6334949"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6334949\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6334949\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t<div class="reply"><a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/?replytocom=6334949#respond" data-commentid="6334949" data-postid="466450" data-belowelement="div-comment-6334949" data-respondelement="respond" data-replyto="Reply to Dude" aria-label="Reply to Dude">Reply</a></div>\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t<ol class="children">\n\t<li id="comment-6334951" class="comment odd alt depth-5  max-depth">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6334951" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Dude</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6334951">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-27T06:05:30-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 27, 2021 at 6:05 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>Of course here as well, the ideal 555 nm source is by definition 100% efficient. It converts energy perfectly to photons and produces light that is perceived the brightest.</p>\n<p>A &#8220;perfect white&#8221; source with no source losses and a color temperature of 5800 K, basically a black body spectrum truncated to the perceivable colors, would have an overall luminous efficiency of 37%. This improves as you make the spectrum narrower. Likewise, LED lights are able to overcome this only by compromising on the light quality with a CRI less than 100.</p>\n<p>If you have a white LED with a claimed luminous efficiency of 75%, I have some news for you: either they&#8217;re lying, or it&#8217;s nowhere near white.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6334951"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6334951\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6334951\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n\t<li id="comment-6334954" class="comment even depth-5  max-depth">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6334954" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Dude</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6334954">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-27T06:17:33-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 27, 2021 at 6:17 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>Or to make it absolutely clear: </p>\n<p>An ideal white LED with 100% source efficiency from electricity to radiation is 38.1\xe2\x80\x9343.9% efficient in terms of visible light.</p>\n<p>All real world LEDs are much worse than this^, with typical source efficiencies in the 20-30% range, which drops the practical luminous efficiency down from the theoretical max 300 lm/W to around 75-100 lm/W and the overall luminous efficiency becomes 10-15%</p>\n<p>^(due to: heat effects, ballast losses, light losses in the LED casing, reflector and diffuser losses&#8230;)</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6334954"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6334954\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6334954\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t</li><!-- #comment-## -->\n</ol><!-- .children -->\n</li><!-- too much, with the exception of yellow fog lights being effective for snow situations.  The convo came up because he was replacing his headlight bulbs to match the color temperature of his new lightbar.  I don&#8217;t remember his explanation of why it was important to try to match color temperatures, but it made sense at the time.  </p>\n<p>Being able to change color temperatures on the fly sounds like a great idea though, and relatively easy to implement &#8211; there are definitely situations where warm white is advantageous (bad weather driving, seeing detail of snow covered roads) and other situations where cool white offers more performance (lets your eyes see further assuming clear skies. href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333517">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>Instead of a laser and its inherent risk, use an electron beam to excite the phosphor. All you need is an electron gun and an electromagnetic focusing ring and some high voltage to attract the electrons and put it in a vacuum and use some lenses to project the light. Very old tech made new and much safer than lasers.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333517"><a I don&#8217;t drive but as a passenger, someimes it seems like<br/>\nI&#8217;m looking at the sun when headlights hit my eyes. Couple this with a recent<br/>\nretina detachment (fixed) and a cataract (yet to be fixed), and these bright<br/>\nlights are just torture. I will say that bicycle lighting has improved since<br/>\nthe days of the little dynamo powered flashlight bulb that was meant for<br/>\ndrivers to see you instead of you lighting up the road in front of you.</p>\n<p><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://youtu.be/9MAkl8tEsnA" rel="nofollow ugc" target="_blank">https://youtu.be/9MAkl8tEsnA</a></p>\n<p>How about low power bulbs coupled with a winshield that amplifies the ambient light<br/>\nwhile blocking direct light?</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333532"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333532\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333532\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t<div class="reply"><a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/?replytocom=6333532#respond" data-commentid="6333532" data-postid="466450" data-belowelement="div-comment-6333532" data-respondelement="respond" data-replyto="Reply to John Q. my SUVs headlights from halogen bulbs with reflector housing to LED projectors (the mid-generation refresh of my car included projector headlights and LED tail lights, so my &#8220;upgrade&#8221; was OEM.  I absolutely hated the cut-off line and switched back to good old reflector housings.  Because my SUV is tall to begin with and lifted 2&#8243;, I had to angle the beam path down to not blind cars when I pulled up behind them at a red light.  This killed the my down road range, making everything close to me brightly illuminated but the stuff down the road that I needed to see was completely black.  </p>\n<p>I dont see why everyone is so fascinated with focusing light in such a way that results in sharp cut-off regions.  I see it with flashlights and headlamps too.  Turn the intensity down and stick with a more natural distribution of the light!</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content -->\n\t\t\t<div class="report-abuse">\n                \n\t\t\t<span id="safe-comments-result-6333542"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm(\'Are you sure you want to report this comment?\')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( \'6333542\', \'84066847aa\', \'safe-comments-result-6333542\'):\'\';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>\n\t\t\t<div class="reply"><a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/?replytocom=6333542#respond" data-commentid="6333542" data-postid="466450" data-belowelement="div-comment-6333542" data-respondelement="respond" data-replyto="Reply to DC" aria-label="Reply to DC">Reply</a></div>\t\t</article><!-- .comment-body -->\n\t<ol class="children">\n\t<li id="comment-6333666" class="comment odd alt depth-2  ">\n\t\t<article id="div-comment-6333666" class="comment-body">\n\t\t\t<footer class="comment-meta">\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-author vcard">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<b class="fn">Martin</b> <span class="says">says:</span>\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-author -->\n\n\t\t\t\t<div class="comment-metadata">\n\t\t\t\t\t<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333666">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<time datetime="2021-03-23T07:55:59-07:00">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\tMarch 23, 2021 at 7:55 am\t\t\t\t\t\t</time>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->\n\n\t\t\t<div class="comment-content">\n\t\t\t\t<p>Normally you should adjust low beam lights for a 40m range. You have no one to blame for lowering your new hid lights so you wouldn&#8217;t blind the driver at stops. While well intentioned and being courteous, the lift may be more harm than good. </p>\n<p>As you may or may not know,  hid projector headlights are designed with a cutoff plate inside to cut off the upper beam in a low beam projector otherwise it becomes a high beam to blind everyone. You don&#8217;t have to believe me. Do your research on hid projector lighting. Some euro vehicles have this cutoff plate on a solenoid, effectively allowing the low beam to become a high beam when the cutoff plate is moved out of the way.</p>\n\t\t\t</div><!-- .comment-content Question 2

  1. Read in your article's html source from the file you created in question 1 and print it's text (use .get_text())
In[4]:
import pickle
from bs4 import BeautifulSoup

filename = 'article-html.pkl'

#Open the file in read mode
with open(filename,'rb') as file:
    article_content = pickle.load(file)

soup = BeautifulSoup(article_content, 'html.parser')

#Print the content
article_text = soup.get_text()
print(article_text)

 March 27, 2021 






How Laser Headlights Work


                130 Comments            

by:
Lewin Day



March 22, 2021








When we think about the onward march of automotive technology, headlights arent usually the first thing that come to mind. Engines, fuel efficiency, and the switch to electric power are all more front of mind. However, that doesnt mean there arent thousands of engineers around the world working to improve the state of the art in automotive lighting day in, day out.
Sealed beam headlights gave way to more modern designs once regulations loosened up, while bulbs moved from simple halogens to xenon HIDs and, more recently, LEDs. Now, a new technology is on the scene, with lasers!

Laser Headlights?!
BWMs prototype laser headlight assemblies undergoing testing.
The first image brought to mind by the phrase laser headlights is that of laser beams firing out the front of an automobile. Obviously, coherent beams of monochromatic light would make for poor illumination outside of a very specific spot quite some distance away. Thankfully for our eyes, laser headlights dont work in this way at all.
Instead, laser headlights consist of one or more solid state laser diodes mounted inside the headlight. These blue lasers are fired at a yellow phosphor, similar to that used in white LEDs. This produces a powerful, vibrant white light that can then be bounced off reflectors and out of the headlights towards the road. Laser headlights built in this way have several benefits. Theyre more energy efficient than LEDs that put out the same amount of light, while also being more space efficient, too.
BWMs futuristic i8 was one of the first vehicles to ship with laser headlight technology.
Laser headlights are still a nascent technology, thus far only appearing in a few BMW, Audi, and other select vehicles. BMWs technology was developed in partnership with lighting experts OSRAM. In practice, a regular LED low-beam lamp is used, with the laser used to create an incredibly bright and focused spot, used for high-beams. This can provide illumination out to 600 meters ahead of the vehicle, double that of conventional LED high beams. The lights use indium gallium nitride diode lasers that were originally used in projectors, with power levels above 1 watt. One of the challenges in implementing such technology in an automotive environment is the need for it to operate at temperature extremes. While research lasers and laser pointers may primarily be used at typical room temperatures, automotive headlights must be able to withstand everything from 40 degrees below zero up to 50 degrees C. Thankfully, the lasers high efficiency means it doesnt have huge heat output of its own to further complicate things. Other engineering challenges involve tailoring the optics package for the rough-and-tumble, high vibration environment found in the automotive application. Its also important to ensure, as with any such device, that the end user cant be exposed to harmful laser radiation in the event of accident or malfunction.
Tearing Down the Laser Headlight
A marketing image showing the construction of an aftermarket LED/laser headlight. Wed take the laser power with a grain of salt  its difficult to imagine a 10 W laser shining directly on some small LEDs without melting a hole through the board in short order.
An aftermarket has sprung up too, with delightfully innovative designs. Combined laser/LED headlights are readily available on Alibaba, designed as a drop in replacement for projector lamps on existing vehicles. These often use an LED low-beam, and a combined LED/laser high beam, where the laser diode shoots directly at the LED phosphor to excite it further, rather than using its own. These units often also come with fan cooling to keep the laser and LEDs below their maximum operational temperature. Such developments are exciting, though its important to be wary of the performance of unknown aftermarket headlights. Many aftermarket LED headlight upgrades fail to pass muster when it comes to real-world performance, and theres no reason to believe hybrid LED/laser designs will be any different. Wed love to pass a selection of these parts through a full IIHS test protocol, but thats sadly beyond the scope (and budget!) of this article.
However, [mikeselectricstuff] has happened to lay his hands on both the BMW and aftermarket parts, tearing them all down in his workshop to see what makes them tick. The differences are multitude when laid bare on the bench. The AliExpress part is relatively simple, wired up no differently from a regular headlight. Interestingly, however, the laser high-beam circuit runs all the time in these parts. To prevent blinding other road users, a shutter is kept in place to block the light, which is moved out of the way with a solenoid when the driver turns on the high beam switch.
Where the aftermarket part is a little out of left field, the BMW design is another thing entirely. The cutting-edge headlights are hooked up with multiple connectors and over 30 conductors, with much of the driver electronics living in an external controller. Much of this is to drive the various LEDs and stepper motors for slewing the headlights when steering. However, the laser assembly brings its own complexities. Twin light sensors are built inside to monitor the laser beam, and a special metal blocking arm sits directly in front of the diode, presumably to stop the laser light leaving the headlight in the event the phosphor coating burns through. Its truly wild to get a look inside a modern luxury cars headlight and see just how far weve come from the old days of simple sealed beams.
Cost Versus Performance
Despite the efficiency gains available, the technology remains expensive. Powerful laser diodes dont come cheap, after all. However, as the technology trickles down to lower-end models, its likely that we could see economies of scale change that for the better. Indeed, if national authorities begin to demand higher performance headlights as standard, we could see laser headlights become the norm, rather than an expensive luxury. The technology could naturally be applied to home and commercial lighting, too  though we suspect the potential gains are limited enough that LED lighting will remain the norm for some time to come.
The high light output of laser headlights in a compact package allows engineers greater freedom when designing the front-end of a car.
As it currently stands, much of the appeal of the new technology is about the packaging benefits, which allow automotive designers greater freedom around the headlight area. Such concerns are less of a factor when it comes to light fittings in the home or office, or indeed, on lower-end automobiles. Regardless, its an exciting new application for lasers and one well be sure to see more of in the future.





 



















Posted in car hacks, Engineering, Featured, Laser Hacks, SliderTagged laser, laser headlight, laser headlights, light

Dude says: 



							March 22, 2021 at 10:04 am						




I dont think so. An average car can do a hard stop within 70 meters from 70 kph (19.5 m/s) and thats giving you two seconds of reaction time. Assuming linear deceleration, your average stopping time will be between 7-8 seconds. If you take twice that time, your brakes are shot.
Second point is, assuming average eyesight, the smallest distinct object you can just about make out from 600 meters away would be the size of a football, if it has enough contrast with the background, but that would require daylight levels of illumination, and no vibration and shaking from the vehicle. In the dark, no matter how bright your headlights are, you would be lucky to make out an object the size of a truck at that distance. Its just gonna be a blur.

Twin light sensors are built inside to monitor the laser beam, and a special metal blocking arm sits directly in front of the diode, presumably to stop the laser light leaving the headlight in the event the phosphor coating burns through.

jack324 says: 



							March 22, 2021 at 8:52 am						




The arm is fixed, and is almost certainly hefty enough to absorb 1-10w if laser energy without heating up all that much, so no burn through. Also, theres no misalignment if the the laser housing is placed in a premachined or cast part with no option for adjusting the alignment. So basically as is designed, if the phosphor coating is destroyed and the arm isnt a mirror, then all that happens is the lasert light gets scattered around the headlight interior and at best some leaks out but due to light spreading out, unless you where right up on the spot where the phosphor was you wouldnt get enough laser energy hitting your eye to do any damage.
Stop worrying about imagined danger when there isnt any.

Why should one use UV when intense blue light is available? UV has higher quantum energy than blue, which has to be dropped by the fluorescent phosphor to different VIS wavelengths. So blue can be more efficient than UV  at the stage of the phosphor. Not to mention: blue LEDs are very efficient, UV LEDS are not.

three_d_dave says: 



							March 23, 2021 at 3:00 pm						




Today they are for high beams. Then it will be for safety that it is transitioned to low beams. 
Recall that the SAE standards for low-beams assumed that the maximum illumination would be in the central area aligned with the filament, with fall-off to the edges to avoid a sharp transition, and then lamp makers used optics to drive the maximum allowable illumination to 100% of the field with no gradual drop-off, creating that blinding flash transition so popular today. Of course they also did that test with the mandated 12V supply even though autos run theirs at 14-15V, for a gain of around 20-40% in brightness over the test condition. At least with LEDs that lie no longer works, but the elimination of gradual falloff more than makes up. And, the original lamps had a spectrum with little blue in it; now that component that destroys night vision, is a large component.

rnjacobs says:

You had this same misconception last time we talked about light efficiency, and youre still wrong. That 44% figure is efficacy, not efficiency. That means it is relative to an single-color 550nm (yellow-green) light source and you wouldnt want to light a room with that anyway.


Report comment 
Reply 





Dude says: 



							March 27, 2021 at 5:52 am						




No. You got it exactly wrong the last time. Efficacy refers to the number of lumens per Watt of the radiation, and luminous efficiency refers to the perceived brightness relative to 555 nm light. Then there is the overall luminous efficacy and efficiency of the source:
https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luminous_efficacy#Lighting_efficiency
The main difference between the luminous efficacy of radiation and the luminous efficacy of a source is that the latter accounts for input energy that is lost as heat or otherwise
The following table lists luminous efficacy of a source and efficiency for various light sources
Theoretical limit for a white LED with phosphorescence color mixing 260300 lm/W 38.143.9% 
This IS counting watts photons visible light out   watts electrons in.


Report comment 
Reply 





Dude says: 



							March 27, 2021 at 6:05 am						




Of course here as well, the ideal 555 nm source is by definition 100% efficient. It converts energy perfectly to photons and produces light that is perceived the brightest.
A perfect white source with no source losses and a color temperature of 5800 K, basically a black body spectrum truncated to the perceivable colors, would have an overall luminous efficiency of 37%. This improves as you make the spectrum narrower. Likewise, LED lights are able to overcome this only by compromising on the light quality with a CRI less than 100.
If you have a white LED with a claimed luminous efficiency of 75%, I have some news for you: either theyre lying, or its nowhere near white.


Report comment 






Dude says: 



							March 27, 2021 at 6:17 am						




Or to make it absolutely clear: 
An ideal white LED with 100% source efficiency from electricity to radiation is 38.143.9% efficient in terms of visible light.
All real world LEDs are much worse than this^, with typical source efficiencies in the 20-30% range, which drops the practical luminous efficiency down from the theoretical max 300 lm/W to around 75-100 lm/W and the overall luminous efficiency becomes 10-15%
^(due to: heat effects, ballast losses, light losses in the LED casing, reflector and diffuser losses)

You dont have to believe me. Do your research on hid projector lighting. Some euro vehicles have this cutoff plate on a solenoid, effectively allowing the low beam to become a high beam when the cutoff plate is moved out of the way.
